        <title>Whole genomes and transcriptomes reveal adaptation and domestication of pistachio | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Pistachio (Pistacia vera), one of the most important commercial nut crops worldwide, is highly adaptable to abiotic stresses and is tolerant to drought and salt stresses. Here, we provide a draft de novo genome of pistachio as well as large-scale genome resequencing. Comparative genomic analyses reveal stress adaptation of pistachio is likely attributable to the expanded cytochrome P450 and chitinase gene families. Particularly, a comparative transcriptomic analysis shows that the jasmonic acid (JA) biosynthetic pathway plays an important role in salt tolerance in pistachio. Moreover, we resequence 93 cultivars and 14 wild P. vera genomes and 35 closely related wild Pistacia genomes, to provide insights into population structure, genetic diversity, and domestication. We find that frequent genetic admixture occurred among the different wild Pistacia species. Comparative population genomic analyses reveal that pistachio was domesticated about 8000&#8201;years ago and suggest that key genes for domestication related to tree and seed size experienced artificial selection. Our study provides insight into genetic underpinning of local adaptation and domestication of pistachio. The Pistacia genome sequences should facilitate future studies to understand the genetic basis of agronomically and environmentally related traits of desert crops."/>

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Whole genomes and transcriptomes reveal adaptation and domestication of pistachio"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Pistachio (Pistacia vera), one of the most important commercial nut crops worldwide, is highly adaptable to abiotic stresses and is tolerant to drought and salt stresses. Here, we provide a draft de novo genome of pistachio as well as large-scale genome resequencing. Comparative genomic analyses reveal stress adaptation of pistachio is likely attributable to the expanded cytochrome P450 and chitinase gene families. Particularly, a comparative transcriptomic analysis shows that the jasmonic acid (JA) biosynthetic pathway plays an important role in salt tolerance in pistachio. Moreover, we resequence 93 cultivars and 14 wild P. vera genomes and 35 closely related wild Pistacia genomes, to provide insights into population structure, genetic diversity, and domestication. We find that frequent genetic admixture occurred among the different wild Pistacia species. Comparative population genomic analyses reveal that pistachio was domesticated about 8000years ago and suggest that key genes for domestication related to tree and seed size experienced artificial selection. Our study provides insight into genetic underpinning of local adaptation and domestication of pistachio. The Pistacia genome sequences should facilitate future studies to understand the genetic basis of agronomically and environmentally related traits of desert crops."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Pistachio (<i>Pistacia vera</i>), one of the most important commercial nut crops worldwide, is highly adaptable to abiotic stresses and is tolerant to drought and salt stresses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we provide a draft de novo genome of pistachio as well as large-scale genome resequencing. Comparative genomic analyses reveal stress adaptation of pistachio is likely attributable to the expanded cytochrome P450 and chitinase gene families. Particularly, a comparative transcriptomic analysis shows that the jasmonic acid (JA) biosynthetic pathway plays an important role in salt tolerance in pistachio. Moreover, we resequence 93 cultivars and 14 wild <i>P. vera</i> genomes and 35 closely related wild <i>Pistacia</i> genomes, to provide insights into population structure, genetic diversity, and domestication. We find that frequent genetic admixture occurred among the different wild <i>Pistacia</i> species. Comparative population genomic analyses reveal that pistachio was domesticated about 8000years ago and suggest that key genes for domestication related to tree and seed size experienced artificial selection.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Our study provides insight into genetic underpinning of local adaptation and domestication of pistachio. The <i>Pistacia</i> genome sequences should facilitate future studies to understand the genetic basis of agronomically and environmentally related traits of desert crops.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>With reducing agricultural acreage and human population growth, feeding the world is becoming an increasing problem. Deserts take up about one third of the land surface area of Earth, are extreme environments that are barren landscapes where little precipitation occurs, and often have dry and alkaline soils, thus have hostile living conditions for most plant and animal life [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Walker AS. What is a desert? In: Deserts: geology and resources. General Interest Publication. 1992;36" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1768e757">1</a>]. However, some crops can still be cultivated in some desert areas. Insight into the environmental adaptations and economic characters of these species should facilitate the planting and breeding of these crops in different desert regions, which might contribute to easing the worlds food crisis.</p><p>Pistachio (<i>P. vera</i>, 2<i>n</i>=30, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig1">1</a>a) belongs to the Eudicots clade, Sapindales order, and Anacardiaceae family and is a member of the cashew family originating from Central Asia and the Middle East. It is a desert plant that is highly tolerant of saline soil. Pistachio nuts have recently become the fifth largest nut crop, with around 1024&nbsp;kt harvested in 2015 (FAOSTAT. Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations Database, <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>). Iran and the USA were the major producers of pistachios, together accounting for 72.65% of the total world production in 2015, with the USA overtaking Iran in 2016 to become the country with the biggest pistachio production (FAOSTAT). In addition to its economic, nutritional, and medicinal values, pistachio is highly adaptable to abiotic stresses and is considered to be a species that tolerates drought and salt stresses, making it ideal for reforestation of arid and salinized zones [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Moazzzam Jazi M, Seyedi SM, Ebrahimie E, Ebrahimi M, De Moro G, Botanga C. A genome-wide transcriptome map of pistachio (Pistacia vera L.) provides novel insights into salinity-related genes and marker discovery. BMC Genomics. 2017;18:627." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1768e779">2</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="381"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Pistachio genome evolution. <b>a</b> Tree, flower, immature seeds, and mature seeds of pistachio Ghazvini. <b>b</b> Example microsynteny analysis indicating that no lineage-specific whole genome duplication occurred in pistachio. Microcollinearity patterns between genomic regions from Amborella, pistachio and the populus. Rectangles represent predicted gene models, with blue and green showing relative gene orientations. Gray ribbons connect the matching gene pairs. <b>c</b> Expansion (red numbers) and contraction (blue numbers) of gene families in different plants</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although the rapid development of genome sequencing has facilitated to discover genetic underpinning of many crop domestication and improvement, there are very few studies on pistachio. The genome size of pistachio has been estimated to be about 600Mb with a high heterozygosity rate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ziya Motalebipour E, Kafkas S, Khodaeiaminjan M, oban N, Gzel H. Genome survey of pistachio (Pistacia vera L.) by next generation sequencing: development of novel SSR markers and genetic diversity in Pistacia species. BMC Genomics. 2016;17:998." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1768e813">3</a>]. Moazzzam Jazi et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Moazzzam Jazi M, Seyedi SM, Ebrahimie E, Ebrahimi M, De Moro G, Botanga C. A genome-wide transcriptome map of pistachio (Pistacia vera L.) provides novel insights into salinity-related genes and marker discovery. BMC Genomics. 2017;18:627." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1768e816">2</a>] used a genome-wide transcriptome and discovered the salinity tolerance-related markers and stress response mechanisms by comparing two pistachio cultivars under control and salt treatment.</p><p>In the present study, to better understand the molecular evolutionary history underpinning pistachio domestication, we assembled a draft genome of pistachio and resequenced 107 whole genomes, including 93 domestic and 14 wild individuals of <i>P. vera</i> and 35 other genomes from different wild <i>Pistacia</i> species. Integrating genomic and transcriptomic analyses revealed expanded gene families (e.g., cytochrome P450 and chitinase) and the jasmonic acid (JA) biosynthetic pathway that are likely involved in stress adaptation. Comparative population genomic analyses revealed that pistachio was domesticated ~8000years ago and that likely key genes for domestication are those involved in tree and seed size, which experienced artificial selection (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>). These genome sequences should facilitate future studies to understand the genetic underpinnings of agronomically and environmentally related traits of desert crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome evolution of pistachio</h3><p>We firstly sequenced the genome of the <i>P. vera</i> L cultivar by Illumina Hiseq 2500 platform from multiple paired-end libraries, including two small-insert libraries (270bp and 500bp) and six long-insert mate-pair libraries (3kb, 4kb, 8kb, 10kb, 15kb, and 17kb). A draft genome of 569.12Mb was assembled, with contig and scaffold N50 sizes of 20.69kb and 768.39kb, respectively. (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S1-S2, version1). To improve the continuity, we further generated a total of 4,038,150 filtered long reads with average lengths of 14,568bp from 59&nbsp;Gb sequencing data by Pacbio Sequel System. Finally, a draft genome of 671Mb was assembled, with contig and scaffold N50 sizes of 75.7kb and 949.2kb, respectively (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S2, version 2). The genome quality is compatible with the previously reported plant genomes (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S2) and facilitates some convincing data analyses. The assembly size is a little larger than the estimated genome size, which is likely due to the high heterozygosity of pistachio (1.72%). The pattern has also been reported in other assembled genomes with high heterozygosity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang X, Yue Y, Li H, Ding W, Chen G, Shi T, Chen J, Park MS, Chen F, Wang L. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Horticulture Res. 2018;5:72." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1768e855">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wei C, Yang H, Wang S, Zhao J, Liu C, Gao L, Xia E, Lu Y, Tai Y, She G, et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2018;115:E4151." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1768e855_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yan L, Wang X, Liu H, Tian Y, Lian J, Yang R, Hao S, Wang X, Yang S, Li Q, et al. The Genome of Dendrobium officinale illuminates the biology of the important traditional Chinese orchid herb. Mol Plant. 2015;8:92234." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1768e859">6</a>]. Transposable elements occupied 70.7% of the pistachio genome, of which 46.75% were long terminal repeat retrotransposons (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3). A total of 31,784 protein-coding genes and 161 miRNAs were annotated by integrating different methods (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S4-S5). Conserved Core Eukaryotic Gene Mapping Approach (CEGMA) analyses indicated that 96.94% of the core protein-coding genes were recovered in our assembled genome. The assembly sequence was assessed with BUSCO v3.0.2b which found 1361 complete gene models out of 1440 (94.51%) and 29 fragmented (2.01%); 18.96% of complete genes were found in more than one copy (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S6).</p><p>We first performed a comparative genomic investigation to assess the palaeohistory of this species. Phylogenomic analysis using genes extracted from single-copy families in nine plant genomes indicated that the pistachio diverged from <i>Citrus sinensis</i> ~58 million years ago and from <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> ~105 million years ago (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1). Analysis of fourfold degenerate third-codon transversion sites demonstrated that the pistachio genome had not experienced a lineage-specific whole genome duplication subsequent to its divergence from these species (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S2). We also performed a genomic synteny analysis by aligning the pistachio genome to the genome of the basal angiosperm <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science. 2013;342:1241089." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1768e890">7</a>]. The macrosynteny analyses showed that each <i>Amborella</i> region had up to three pistachio regions, while each pistachio region had up to two <i>P. trichocarpa</i> regions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tuskan GA, DiFazio S, Jansson S, Bohlmann J, Grigoriev I, Hellsten U, Putnam N, Ralph S, Rombauts S, Salamov A, et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science. 2006;313:1596604." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1768e899">8</a>] (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig1">1</a>b, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S3). The synteny analyses support the conclusion that no lineage-specific genome duplication occurred in pistachio, but they do share the gamma duplication that occurred within eudicots, and that <i>Populus</i> experienced a lineage-specific genome duplication event [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science. 2013;342:1241089." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1768e912">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tuskan GA, DiFazio S, Jansson S, Bohlmann J, Grigoriev I, Hellsten U, Putnam N, Ralph S, Rombauts S, Salamov A, et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science. 2006;313:1596604." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1768e915">8</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Expanded gene families related with stress adaptation of pistachio</h3><p>To reveal the genetic basis underpinning the pistachio phenotype (e.g., salt tolerance), we investigated the evolution of gene families by identifying unique and shared gene families among different plants using OrthoMCL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ Jr, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:217889." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1768e926">9</a>]. Among the gene families identified in <i>P. vera</i>, 9735 were shared as families with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>Gossypium raimondii</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, while 707 gene families, containing 1381 genes, were specific to pistachio (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S4). To assess the function of these genes, we performed a Gene Ontology (GO) and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) enrichment analysis using David and g:Profiler programs. Both programs found many genes involved in defense response (GO: 0006952, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S7), which included many genes containing the NB-ARC domain and the NBS-LRR domain. This kind of genes has been well known for disease resistance in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="McHale L, Tan X, Koehl P, Michelmore RW. Plant NBS-LRR proteins: adaptable guards. Genome Biol. 2006;7:212." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1768e951">10</a>] and is reasonably important for defense response in pistachio.</p><p>Next, we examined the expansion and contraction of gene families in pistachio (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig1">1</a>c). As it is difficult to reach conclusions concerning adaptation from contraction in gene family size, or with genes that were not successfully assembled in this reference genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wang M-S, Yang H-C, Otecko NO, Wu D-D, Zhang Y-P. Olfactory genes in Tibetan wild boar. Nat Genet. 2016;48:972." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1768e960">11</a>], we only analyzed the expanded gene families. Gene enrichment analysis of the expanded gene families found them to be enriched in the categories of metabolism, such as biosynthesis of terpenoid, flavonoid, sesquiterpenoid, and alkaloid (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S8). The expansion of gene families occurs after a long-term evolution and drives the evolutionary difference between <i>Pistacia</i> and <i>Citrus</i>, rather than a very short-term evolution of pistachio domestication from the wild. Therefore, we propose that the expansion of genes in the above categories is probably related to the metabolism of organic compounds found in wild <i>Pistacia</i> species. Phytochemical screening of wild <i>Pistacia</i> species found many phytochemicals such as alkaloids, flavonoids, coumarins, sterols, tannins, terpenoids, and sesquiterpene [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Uddin G, Rauf A, Rehman T, Qaisar M. Phytochemical screening of Pistacia chinensis var. integerrima. Middle-East J Sci Res. 2011;7:70711." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1768e979">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Belhachat D, Aid F, Mekimene L, Belhachat M. Phytochemical screening and in vitro antioxidant activity of Pistacia lentiscus berries ethanolic extract growing in Algeria. Mediterr J Nutr Metab. 2017;10:27385." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1768e979_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Rand K, Bar E, Ben-Ari M, Lewinsohn E, Inbar M. The mono - and sesquiterpene content of aphid-induced galls on Pistacia palaestina is not a simple reflection of their composition in intact leaves. J Chem Ecol. 2014;40:63242." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1768e982">14</a>].</p><p>In addition, the enriched term oxidation-reduction process (GO: 0055114, <i>P</i> <span class="stix"></span> 0.001) contains many cytochrome P450 genes, which encode proteins involved in multiple metabolic pathways with complex functions and playing important roles in multiple processes, particularly roles in stress responses. To assess the function of these genes, we used BLASTP to search the <i>A. thaliana</i> proteome and identified the best-hit genes (<i>E</i> value &lt;1e10) (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S9). Among the 187 cytochrome P450 genes, we found that many probably had functions for salt tolerance. For example, pervious study found elevated levels of <i>CYP94</i> family gene expression alleviate the jasmonate response and enhance salt tolerance in rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ogawa D, Kurotani K-I, Hayashi K, Hatanaka S, Hattori T, Toda Y, Takeda S, Ichikawa H, Ueda M, Tashita R, et al. Elevated levels of CYP94 family gene expression alleviate the jasmonate response and enhance salt tolerance in rice. Plant Cell Physiol. 2015;56:77989." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1004">15</a>]. Among these expanded gene families in pistachio, there are 14 members of <i>CYP94</i> genes. In soybean, <i>CYP82A3</i> is involved in the jasmonic acid and ethylene signaling pathway and enhances resistance to salinity and drought [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Yan Q, Cui X, Lin S, Gan S, Xing H, Dou D. GmCYP82A3, a soybean cytochrome P450 family gene involved in the jasmonic acid and ethylene signaling pathway, enhances plant resistance to biotic and abiotic stresses. Plos One. 2016;11:e0162253." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1013">16</a>], and there are 20 members of <i>CYP82</i> genes among the expanded gene families in pistachio. Ectopic expression of <i>P. trichocarpa CYP714A3</i> enhanced salt tolerance in rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang C, Yang Y, Wang H, Ran X, Li B, Zhang J, Zhang H. Ectopic expression of a cytochrome P450 monooxygenase gene PtCYP714A3 from Populus trichocarpa reduces shoot growth and improves tolerance to salt stress in transgenic rice. Plant Biotechnol J. 2016;14:183851." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1023">17</a>], and there are 10 members of <i>CYP714A</i> genes among the expanded gene families in pistachio. Thus, it is likely that some cytochrome P450 genes are responsible for salt tolerance in pistachio.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">RNA sequencing reveals a genetic mechanism underlying salt adaptation of pistachio</h3><p>To further investigate the genetic mechanisms underlying salt tolerance in pistachio, we performed a salinity experiment. Leaf and root transcriptomes of pistachio rootstock, <i>P. vera L.</i> cv. Ohadi, grown under normal and salinity conditions (see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Sec10">Methods</a> section) were generated by RNA sequencing. Using the Tophat-Cufflinks-Cuffdiff pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Trapnell C, Roberts A, Goff L, Pertea G, Kim D, Kelley DR, Pimentel H, Salzberg SL, Rinn JL, Pachter L. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat Protoc. 2012;7:562." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1043">18</a>], 214 and 461 protein-coding genes were identified to exhibit differential expression, respectively in leaf and root tissues (<i>n</i><sub>control</sub>=3, <i>n</i><sub>salinity</sub>=3, corrected <i>P</i>&lt;0.05, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S5), between plants treated under saline conditions versus the control. Gene enrichment analysis found that many of the differentially expressed genes (31 genes) are involved in oxidation-reduction process (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig2">2</a>a, b; Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S10-S11). As in the comparative genomic analysis, 15 genes in this category are cytochrome P450 genes, specifically, <i>CYP74A</i> (i.e., <i>AOS</i>), which encodes a member of the cytochrome P450 CYP74 gene family that functions as an allene oxide synthase (AOS). This enzyme catalyzes the first committed step in the synthesis of jasmonates (i.e., jasmonic acid (JA)) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Park JH, Halitschke R, Kim HB, Baldwin IT, Feldmann KA, Feyereisen R. A knock-out mutation in allene oxide synthase results in male sterility and defective wound signal transduction in Arabidopsis due to a block in jasmonic acid biosynthesis. Plant J. 2002;31:112." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1074">19</a>]. The expression fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped (FPKM) values for <i>AOS</i> increased from nearly 0 in the control to 2163.75 under saline conditions in the leaf, and from 1.87 in the control to 87.74 for the saline-treated root (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig2">2</a>c). We also found that 7 differentially expressed genes (<i>ChiC</i>, <i>TT4</i>, <i>ILL6</i>, <i>MYB108</i>, <i>MYB6</i>, <i>PRB1</i>, and <i>TIFY5A</i>) were enriched for response to jasmonic acid (<i>P</i>=0.005 after correction; Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig2">2</a>c, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S10-S11). Previous studies have shown that both drought and high salinity caused increased JA levels in the leaves and roots of rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Moons A, Prinsen E, Bauw G, Van Montagu M. Antagonistic effects of abscisic acid and jasmonates on salt stress-inducible transcripts in rice roots. Plant Cell. 1997;9:224359." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1115">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Du H, Liu H, Xiong L. Endogenous auxin and jasmonic acid levels are differentially modulated by abiotic stresses in rice. Front Plant Sci. 2013;4:397." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1118">21</a>]. Salinity treatment can increase endogenous JA level in the <i>Iris hexagona</i>, a wetland species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Wang Y, Mopper S, Hasenstein KH. Effects of salinity on endogenous Aba, Iaa, Ja, and Sa in Iris hexagona. J Chem Ecol. 2001;27:32742." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1124">22</a>]. Jasmonates activate plant responses to biotic stresses (i.e., attack by pathogens) and abiotic stresses (i.e., salt) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wasternack C, Hause B. A bypass in jasmonate biosynthesis-the OPR3-independent formation. Trends Plant Sci. 2018;S13601385(1318):300426." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1128">23</a>]. Here, expression levels of these genes involved in response to jasmonic acid are increased in the leaf and root with the saline treatment (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig2">2</a>c). The increased expressions of these genes (e.g., AOS as enzyme catalyzing the first committed step in the synthesis of jasmonates) should increase the synthesis of jasmonates, and thus, they are likely used by pistachio to respond to salt stress.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="565"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Transcriptome analysis of saline-treated pistachio. <b>a</b> Expression heatmap of genes in the oxidation-reduction process. C, control pistachio leaf; S, saline-treated pistachio leaf. <i>n</i><sub>control</sub>=3, <i>n</i><sub>salinity</sub>=3. Expression values were normalized by log2 (FPKM+1). <b>b</b> Expression values (FPKM) of <i>CYP74A</i> in the leaf (left) and root (right). <b>c</b> Relative expression levels of seven genes in the category response to jasmonic acid</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Differentially expressed genes were also found to be enriched in chitin binding (<i>P</i>=0.03 after correction, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S10), with four genes encoding chitinases (<i>CHIB</i>, <i>EP3</i>, <i>ChiC</i>, <i>AT2G43590</i>). Plant chitinases are involved in diverse biological systems. Some chitinases in plants are expressed in response to environmental stresses (i.e., high salt concentration, cold, and drought) and can be upregulated by phytohormones such as ethylene, jasmonic acid, and salicylic acid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kasprzewska A. Plant chitinases--regulation and function. Cell Mol Biol Lett. 2003;8:80924." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1195">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Grover A. Plant chitinases: genetic diversity and physiological roles AU - Grover, Anita. Crit Rev Plant Sci. 2012;31:5773." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1198">25</a>]. For example, gene <i>ChiC</i> encodes a class V chitinase, and its expression can be induced by the jasmonic acid and the stress resulting from salinity in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ohnuma T, Numata T, Osawa T, Mizuhara M, Lampela O, Juffer AH, Skriver K, Fukamizo T. A class V chitinase from Arabidopsis thaliana: gene responses, enzymatic properties, and crystallographic analysis. Planta. 2011;234:12337." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1207">26</a>]. <i>CHIB</i> encodes a basic chitinase involved in jasmonic acid-mediated signaling pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zander M, La Camera S, Lamotte O, Mtraux J-P, Gatz C. Arabidopsis thaliana class-II TGA transcription factors are essential activators of jasmonic acid/ethylene-induced defense responses. Plant J. 2010;61:20010." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1214">27</a>]. Our transcriptomic analysis suggests that genes encoding chitinases and those involved in the JA biosynthetic pathway likely contribute to the adaptation of pistachio to saline environments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Admixture occurred among different wild relatives</h3><p>To investigate the demographic history and adaptive evolution of pistachio, we resequenced an additional 107 genomes from <i>P. vera</i> including 93 cultivars and 14 genomes of wild pistachio to an average depth of 6~8. We also resequenced 35 genomes from different close species, including <i>P. mutica</i>, <i>P. khinjuk</i>, <i>P. integerrima</i>, and <i>P. palaestina</i> (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S12-S13). Using a stringent GATK pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="McKenna A, Hanna M, Banks E, Sivachenko A, Cibulskis K, Kernytsky A, Garimella K, Altshuler D, Gabriel S, Daly M, DePristo MA. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 2010;20:1297303." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1244">28</a>], 14.77 million single-base variants were called, with 2.42 million of them being in genic regions (intronic and exonic; 412,917 nonsynonymous, 354,937 synonymous) (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S14-S17, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S6-S9). Phylogenetic analyses using the neighbor joining and maximum likelihood methods clearly separated the 5 different species, i.e., <i>P. vera</i>, <i>P. mutica</i>, <i>P. khinjuk</i>, <i>P. integerrima</i>, and <i>P. palaestina</i> (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S10-S11). Signals of introgression were detected between some species by the TreeMix program [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Pickrell JK, Pritchard JK. Inference of population splits and mixtures from genome-wide allele frequency data. PLoS Genet. 2012;8:e1002967." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1272">29</a>], i.e., from <i>P. khinjuk</i> to <i>P. integerrima</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig3">3</a>), which were supported by the ABBA-BABA test (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S12). This indicates that hybridization likely occurs among the different close relatives in nature and is consistent with the pervasive hybridization seen in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Vigueira CC, Olsen KM, Caicedo AL. The red queen in the corn: agricultural weeds as models of rapid adaptive evolution. Heredity. 2012;110:303." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1288">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Vieira J, Vieira CP. On the identification of human selected loci in grapevines. Heredity. 2009;104:327." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1291">31</a>]. However, no introgression was detected from other pistachio species to domesticated pistachio, which was derived from wild <i>P. vera</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="643"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Signal of introgression among different wild species detected by the TreeMix program. Hybridization likely occurs among the different close relatives in nature. However, no introgression was detected from other wild species to cultivated pistachio</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">A two-step domestication of pistachio</h3><p>Based on these resequenced genomes, we inferred the changes in effective population sizes of these species and found a bottleneck event during the Pleistocene period and an increase in their effective population size ~200&nbsp;kyr ago (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13). Our phylogenetic tree shows a clear separation between domestic and wild pistachio (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig4">4</a>a). The divergence time between wild and domestic was inferred by ai to be ~8000years ago (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S13, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S18), which is similar to the archeological record showing that pistachio seeds were a common food as early as 6750BC [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kashaninejad M, Tabil LG: Pistachio (Pistacia vera L.). In Postharvest Biology and Technology of Tropical and Subtropical Fruits. Edited by Yahia EM: Woodhead Publishing. 2011;218247" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1336">32</a>]. To gain insight into the genetic relationships among the pistachio accessions, we performed two classical analyses: population structure and principal component analysis (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig4">4</a>b, c; Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S14). These analyses clearly show two groups of cultivar accessions. The level of linkage disequilibrium (LD) is highest within cultivar group I, while the rate of decay of LD is nearly the same in both cultivar group II and wild pistachio (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S15). Group II includes five individuals from the cultivars Qazvini, Italiaei, and Badami Zarand, which are recorded to be ancient and harboring seeds of small size (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig4">4</a>d). Consistent with the phylogenetic tree, these three cultivars also contained a higher proportion of wild ancestry (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig4">4</a>e), which supports a two-step domestication processes, with initial domestication, followed by improvement through crop breeding.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="467"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Phylogenetic analysis of wild and domestic pistachio. <b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree. Cultivars of group I (red), cultivar group II (green), and wild pistachio (blue) are marked in different colors from top to bottom. <b>b</b> PCA analysis. From left to right, the three squares indicate cultivar group I, cultivar group II, and wild pistachio. <b>c</b> Admixture analysis. From left to right, the three groups, i.e., cultivar group I, cultivar group II, and wild pistachio are marked by different colors. <b>d</b> From left to right, representative dry seeds from cultivar group I, cultivar group II, and wild pistachio. <b>e</b> Correlation between the proportion of cultivar genetic component and dried pistachio fruit weight</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genetic mechanisms underlying pistachio domestication</h3><p>Average pairwise nucleotide diversity within populations (<i></i>) indicated lower diversity among the domestic accessions compared with the wild (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S19). Using a log2 ratio of <i></i> between cultivated and wild samples, we found some genomic regions that showed reduced diversity in the cultivated that may contain genes under artificial selection (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S16). In addition, we identified regions with an increased level of differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) between the cultivated and wild samples (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S16-S17). About 9.2Mb of genomic regions were identified to display high levels of population differentiation between domestic and wild pistachio, and low levels of genetic diversity among cultivars, being above the 95% threshold for both properties (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S16, Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S20-S21). Regions of reduced diversity and enhanced population differentiation might have experienced selective sweeps during domestication or breeding. In total, 665 genes were located in these regions (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S22-S24). We focused on candidate positively selected genes that might be associated with the evolution of phenotypes important for domestication. The tree size was a target for artificial selection during the domestication of pistachio (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig5">5</a>a). We found the gene <i>SAUR55</i> (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig5">5</a>b), encoding auxin response protein that plays important roles in plant growth [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Guilfoyle J, GretchenHagen T. Auxin response factors. Curr Opin Plant Biol. 2007;10:45360." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1435">33</a>], evolved under artificial selection in pistachio. Particularly, gene <i>SAUR55</i> displays a significantly increased expression level in domestic compared to wild pistachio, based on the leaf and root transcriptome data (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig5">5</a>c). These observations are consistent with evidence of selective sweeps on auxin response genes in other crops, such as rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Xu X, Liu X, Ge S, Jensen JD, Hu F, Li X, Dong Y, Gutenkunst RN, Fang L, Huang L, et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat Biotech. 2011;30:10511." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1444">34</a>] and wheat [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Avni R, Nave M, Barad O, Baruch K, Twardziok SO, Gundlach H, Hale I, Mascher M, Spannagl M, Wiebe K, et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science. 2017;357:937." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1447">35</a>], and reveal convergent artificial selection for similar traits during crop domestication. Fruit weight is among the most important traits targeted during domestication and breeding of crops, including pistachio. A positive correlation was found between the proportion of cultivar component and fruit weight among cultivars (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#Fig4">4</a>e). This supports the conclusion that artificial selection on fruit weight occurred on pistachio during domestication and cultivation. We note that the gene <i>CYCD7-1</i> evolved under artificial selection with a signature of a high level of population differentiation between wild and domestic cultivars (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S18-S19). This gene encodes a D-type cyclin, which controls cell division and growth rate during seed development. Over-expression of <i>CYCD7-1</i> induces cell proliferation and cell enlargement in the embryo and endosperm, leading to the overgrowth of seed, in <i>Arabidopsis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Collins C, Dewitte W, Murray JAH. D-type cyclins control cell division and developmental rate during Arabidopsis seed development. J Exp Bot. 2012;63:357186." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1466">36</a>]. Gene <i>CYCD7-1</i> displays special expression in pollen and early development, but no expression in the leaf and root (Additional&nbsp;file&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S20). Therefore, it is promising to compare expression of <i>CYCD7-1</i> in pollen and early development of wild and domestic pistachio in the future experiment. We propose that artificial selection on <i>CYCD7-1</i> might occur to improve pistachio fruit weight.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs13059-019-1686-3/MediaObjects/13059_2019_1686_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="446"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Artificial selection on pistachio tree size. <b>a</b> From left to right are the photographs of trees of wild pistachio, Badami-zarand cultivar, and Ohadi cultivar; the trees are of the same age and grown under similar conditions at the Pistachio Research Center, Rafsanjan, Iran. The same marker post is located beside each of the trees. <b>b</b> <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>, <i></i>, and Tajimas <i>D</i> value of <i>SAUR55</i> between cultivated and wild pistachio. <b>c</b> Relative expression levels of <i>SAUR55</i> in the leaf and root, <i>n</i><sub>cultivar</sub>=3, <i>n</i><sub>wild</sub>=3</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>In this study, we assembled a draft reference genome and used comparative genomics to reveal the genetic underpinning for salt tolerance adaptation in pistachio, one of the most important commercial nut crops cultivated in desert regions. This reference genome will enable future evolutionary and ecological research on pistachio. We also generated a genome-wide dataset of SNP from 93 domestic and 14 wild individuals and identified some genes for agronomically related traits, such as fruit weight and tree size, that might have been selected during the domestication of pistachio. Sequence information from diverse cultivar accessions should be helpful for the researchers and breeders in genome-wide association mapping studies of agronomically related traits and support marker-assisted and genomic selection-based approaches for plant improvement. Insight into the environmental adaptations and economic characters of this crop will undoubtedly facilitate planting and breeding programs for different desert regions, which might contribute to easing the worlds food crisis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Illumina sequencing for de novo genome</h3><p>An individual of <i>P. vera</i>, cultivar name Batoury, a variety widely cultivated in China, was chosen for genome sequencing and assembly. Genomic DNA was extracted using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen). Multiple pair-end libraries, including two types of small-insert libraries (270bp and 500bp) and six types of long-insert mate-pair libraries (3kb, 4kb, 8kb, 10kb, 15kb, and 17kb), were constructed using the Illumina paired-end and mate-pair kits according to the manufacturers instructions.</p><p>The libraries were sequenced on the Illumina Hiseq 2500 platform. For the raw reads, sequencing adaptors were removed and contaminated reads (chloroplast, mitochondrial, bacterial and viral sequences, etc.) were screened by alignment to the NCBI NR database using BWA v0.7.13 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:175460." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1561">37</a>] with default parameters. FastUniq v1.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Xu H, Luo X, Qian J, Pang X, Song J, Qian G, Chen J, Chen S. FastUniq: a fast de novo duplicates removal tool for paired short reads. PLoS One. 2012;7:e52249." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1564">38</a>] was used to remove duplicated read pairs, and low-quality reads were filtered under the following conditions: (1) reads with 10% unidentified nucleotides (<i>N</i>); (2) reads with &gt;10 nucleotides aligned to the adapter, allowing 10% mismatch; and (3) reads with &gt;50% bases having Phred quality &lt;5. Finally, we generated a total of 98.08Gb and 55.87Gb clean reads for the paired-end and the mate-pair libraries, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">PacBio sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Single-molecule sequencing was performed by the PacBio Sequel System, which yielded a total of 4,038,150 filtered subreads with average lengths of 14,568bp for <i>P. vera</i>. Finally, only equal or longer than 500bp PacBio subreads were performed the gap filling. Based on the ALLPATH-LG assembly, we utilized PBJelly [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="English AC, Richards S, Han Y, Wang M, Vee V, Qu J, Qin X, Muzny DM, Reid JG, Worley KC, Gibbs RA. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One. 2012;7:e47768." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1581">39</a>] to do gap filling with the filtered PacBio subreads for improving genome assembly, and the option is &lt;blasr&gt; -minMatch 8 -minPctIdentity 70 -bestn 1 -nCandidates 20 -maxScore -500 -noSplitSubreads &lt;/blasr&gt;. The final polishing procedure was completed by pilon v1.22 (available at <a href="https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon">https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon</a>) using Illumina data with the parameters --mindepth 10 --changes --threads 4 --fix bases.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome size estimation</h3><p>A total of 58.80Gb Illumina reads from the 270bp library were selected to perform the genome size estimation. The distribution of 21-<i>k</i>mer showed a major peak at 84. Based on the total number <i>k</i>mers and the corresponding <i>k</i>mer depth of 84, the pistachio genome size was estimated to be ~519.17Mb using the formula: Genome size=<i>k</i>mer_Number/Peak_Depth.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">De novo assembly and assessment</h3><p>The whole genome was de novo assembled into longer contigs using ALLPATH-LG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro FJ, Burton JN, Walker BJ, Sharpe T, Hall G, Shea TP, Sykes S, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2011;108:15138." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1619">40</a>] with the default parameters. Because high genomic heterozygosity is generally recovered as alternative contigs, REDUNDANS v.013b [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Pryszcz LP, Gabaldon T. Redundans: an assembly pipeline for highly heterozygous genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44:e113." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1622">41</a>] was used to identify heterozygous contigs based on at least 85% identity and overlap of at least 66% of the shorter sequence length from each pairwise comparison, with only the longer of the two redundant contigs retained. Finally, adjacent contigs connected by mate-pair information were joined to scaffolds using SSPACE v2.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:5789." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1625">42</a>] and gap filling was proceeded by GapCloser v1.12 from the SOAPdenovo package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, He G, Chen Y, Pan Q, Liu Y, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1628">43</a>].</p><p>After detecting the variation using the corresponding sequencing data, the SNP rate besides the genome heterozygosity was estimated as the error rate of the genome assembly. Completeness of the assembly was assessed by mapping 458 conserved core eukaryotic genes (CGEs) and 248 highly conserved CGEs to the genome using CEGMA v2.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:10617." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1634">44</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Repetitive sequences</h4><p>The amount of the assembly composed of repeats was estimated by building a repeat library employing the de novo prediction programs LTR-FINDER [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xu Z, Wang H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:W2658." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1650">45</a>], MITE-Hunter [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Han Y, Wessler SR. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 2010;38:e199." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1653">46</a>], RepeatScout v1.0.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 2005;21(Suppl 1):i3518." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1656">47</a>], and PILER-DF [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Edgar RC, Myers EW. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics. 2005;21(Suppl 1):i1528." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1659">48</a>]. The new repetitive elements were classified using PASTEClassifier v1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wicker T, Sabot F, Hua-Van A, Bennetzen JL, Capy P, Chalhoub B, Flavell A, Leroy P, Morgante M, Panaud O, et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat Rev Genet. 2007;8:97382." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1662">49</a>] and combined with Repbase database v20.01 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bao W, Kojima KK, Kohany O. Repbase update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob DNA. 2015;6:11." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1666">50</a>] to create the final repeat library. Repeat sequences in the pistachio genome were identified and classified using the RepeatMasker program v4.0.6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Tarailo-Graovac M, Chen N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics. 2009;Chapter 4:Unit 4 10." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1669">51</a>]. The criterion used for LTR family classification was that the 5LTR sequence would share at least 80% identity over at least 80% of their length for the same family.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Protein-coding genes</h4><p>Protein-coding genes were predicted using de novo and protein homology-based approaches. Genscan v1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Burge C, Karlin S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J Mol Biol. 1997;268:7894." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1680">52</a>], Augustus v2.5.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stanke M, Waack S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics. 2003;19(Suppl 2):ii21525." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1683">53</a>], GlimmerHMM v3.0.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004;20:28789." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1686">54</a>], GeneID v1.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Blanco E, Parra G, Guig R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics. 2007;4(3):14.3. 28." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1689">55</a>], and SNAP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Korf I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2004;5:59." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1692">56</a>] were performed for de novo gene prediction, while homologous peptides from the <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR 10), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (Nipponbare, IRGSP-1.0), <i>Theobroma cacao</i> (Phytozome v12.1), and <i>C. sinensis</i> (Phytozome v12.1) genomes were aligned to our assembly to identify the homologous genes with GeMoMa v1.4.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Keilwagen J, Wenk M, Erickson JL, Schattat MH, Grau J, Hartung F. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44:e89." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1708">57</a>]; the RNA-Seq reads were assembled into contigs de novo into unigenes using Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:64452." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1711">58</a>], and the resulting unigenes were aligned to the repeat-masked assemblies using BLAT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kent WJ. BLAT--the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 2002;12:65664." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1715">59</a>], and subsequently, the gene structures of BLAT alignment results were modeled using PASA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Smith RK Jr, Hannick LI, Maiti R, Ronning CM, Rusch DB, Town CD, et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003;31:565466." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1718">60</a>]; then, the protein-coding regions were identified with TransDecoder v3.0.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Haas B, Papanicolaou A. TransDecoder (find coding regions within transcripts); 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1721">61</a>] and GeneMarkS-T [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Tang S, Lomsadze A, Borodovsky M. Identification of protein coding regions in RNA transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 2015;43:e78." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1724">62</a>], respectively. Consensus gene models were generated by integrating the de novo predictions and protein alignments using EVidenceModeler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9:R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1727">63</a>]. Annotation of the predicted genes was performed by blasting the gene (and predicted protein) sequences against a number of nucleotide and protein sequence databases, including COG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tatusov RL, Fedorova ND, Jackson JD, Jacobs AR, Kiryutin B, Koonin EV, Krylov DM, Mazumder R, Mekhedov SL, Nikolskaya AN, et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2003;4:41." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1730">64</a>], KEGG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S. KEGG: Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000;28:2730." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-019-1686-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d1768e1734">65</a>], NCBI-NR, and Swiss-Prot [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Boeckmann B, Bairoch A, Apweiler R, Blatter MC, Estreicher A, Gasteiger E, Martin MJ, Michoud K, ODonovan C, Phan I, et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003.
